     <p>
      <b>
       Spider-Man
      </b>
      is a fictional
      <a href="/wiki/Character_(arts)" title="Character (arts)">
       character
      </a>
      , a
      <a href="/wiki/Comic_book" title="Comic book">
       comic book
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Superhero" title="Superhero">
       superhero
      </a>
      that appears in comic books published by
      <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Comics" title="Marvel Comics">
       Marvel Comics
      </a>
      . Created by writer-editor
      <a href="/wiki/Stan_Lee" title="Stan Lee">
       Stan Lee
      </a>
      and writer-artist
      <a href="/wiki/Steve_Ditko" title="Steve Ditko">
       Steve Ditko
      </a>
      , he
      <a href="/wiki/First_appearance" title="First appearance">
       first appeared
      </a>
      in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Amazing_Fantasy" title="Amazing Fantasy">
        Amazing Fantasy
       </a>
      </i>
      #15 (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cover-date" title="Cover-date">
       cover-dated
      </a>
      Aug. 1962). Lee and Ditko conceived the character as an orphan being raised by his
      <a href="/wiki/Aunt_May" title="Aunt May">
       Aunt May
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Uncle_Ben" title="Uncle Ben">
       Uncle Ben
      </a>
      , and as a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teenager" title="Teenager">
       teenager
      </a>
      , having to deal with the normal struggles of adolescence in addition to those of a costumed crimefighter. Spider-Man's creators gave him super strength and agility, the ability to cling to most surfaces, shoot spider-webs using wrist-mounted devices of his own invention (which he called "web-shooters"), and react to danger quickly with his "spider-sense", enabling him to combat his foes.
     </p>
     <p>
      When Spider-Man first appeared in the early 1960s, teenagers in superhero comic books were usually relegated to the role of
      <a href="/wiki/Sidekick" title="Sidekick">
       sidekick
      </a>
      to the
      <a href="/wiki/Protagonist" title="Protagonist">
       protagonist
      </a>
      . The Spider-Man series broke ground by featuring
      <b>
       Peter Parker
      </b>
      , the high school student behind Spider-Man's
      <a href="/wiki/Secret_identity" title="Secret identity">
       secret identity
      </a>
      and with whose "self-obsessions with rejection, inadequacy, and loneliness" young readers could relate.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_3-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wright-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Unlike previous teen heroes such as
      <a href="/wiki/Bucky" title="Bucky">
       Bucky
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Dick_Grayson" title="Dick Grayson">
       Robin
      </a>
      , Spider-Man had no superhero mentor like
      <a href="/wiki/Captain_America" title="Captain America">
       Captain America
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Batman" title="Batman">
       Batman
      </a>
      ; he thus had to learn for himself that "with great power there must also come great responsibility"—a line included in a text box in the final panel of the first Spider-Man story but later
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retcon" title="Retcon">
       retroactively attributed
      </a>
      to his guardian, the late Uncle Ben.
     </p>
     <p>
      Marvel has featured Spider-Man in several
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Spider-Man_titles" title="List of Spider-Man titles">
       comic book series
      </a>
      , the first and longest-lasting of which is titled
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Amazing_Spider-Man" title="The Amazing Spider-Man">
        The Amazing Spider-Man
       </a>
      </i>
      . Over the years, the Peter Parker character has developed from shy, nerdy high school student to troubled but outgoing college student, to married high school teacher to, in the late 2000s, a single freelance photographer, his most typical adult role. In the 2010s, he joins the
      <a href="/wiki/Avengers_(comics)" title="Avengers (comics)">
       Avengers
      </a>
      and the
      <a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Four" title="Fantastic Four">
       Fantastic Four
      </a>
      , Marvel's flagship superhero teams. In a 2012 - 2014 storyline,
      <a href="/wiki/Dying_Wish" title="Dying Wish">
       Peter Parker dies
      </a>
      while his mind is in the body of his enemy
      <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Octopus" title="Doctor Octopus">
       Doctor Octopus
      </a>
      ; Doctor Octopus then lives on inside of Parker's body, taking the role of Spider-Man in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Superior_Spider-Man" title="The Superior Spider-Man">
        The Superior Spider-Man
       </a>
      </i>
      . However, Parker returned to his body in April 2014.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Separately, Marvel has also published books featuring alternate versions of Spider-Man, including
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man_2099" title="Spider-Man 2099">
        Spider-Man 2099
       </a>
      </i>
      , which features the adventures of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miguel_O%27Hara" title="Miguel O'Hara">
       Miguel O'Hara
      </a>
      , the Spider-Man of the future;
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ultimate_Spider-Man" title="Ultimate Spider-Man">
        Ultimate Spider-Man
       </a>
      </i>
      , which features the adventures of a teenaged Peter Parker in
      <a href="/wiki/Ultimate_Marvel" title="Ultimate Marvel">
       an alternate universe
      </a>
      ; and
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ultimate_Comics_Spider-Man" title="Ultimate Comics Spider-Man">
        Ultimate Comics Spider-Man
       </a>
      </i>
      , which depicts the teenager
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miles_Morales" title="Miles Morales">
       Miles Morales
      </a>
      , who takes up the mantle of Spider-Man after Ultimate Peter Parker's death.
     </p>
     <p>
      Spider-Man is one of the most popular and commercially successful superheroes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As Marvel's flagship character and company mascot, he has appeared in many forms of media, including several animated and live-action
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spider-Man_television_series" title="Spider-Man television series">
       television shows
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Print_syndication" title="Print syndication">
       syndicated
      </a>
      newspaper
      <a href="/wiki/The_Amazing_Spider-Man#Newspaper_comic_strip" title="The Amazing Spider-Man">
       comic strips
      </a>
      , and a
      <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man_in_film" title="Spider-Man in film">
       series of films
      </a>
      starring
      <a href="/wiki/Tobey_Maguire" title="Tobey Maguire">
       Tobey Maguire
      </a>
      as the hero in the first three movies.
      <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Garfield" title="Andrew Garfield">
       Andrew Garfield
      </a>
      took over the role of Spider-Man in a
      <a href="/wiki/The_Amazing_Spider-Man_(2012_film)" title="The Amazing Spider-Man (2012 film)">
       reboot of the films
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Reeve_Carney" title="Reeve Carney">
       Reeve Carney
      </a>
      starred as Spider-Man in the 2010
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broadway_musical" title="Broadway musical">
       Broadway musical
      </a>
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man:_Turn_Off_the_Dark" title="Spider-Man: Turn Off the Dark">
        Spider-Man: Turn Off the Dark
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Spider-Man placed 3rd on IGN's Top 100 Comic Book Heroes of All Time in 2011, behind
      <a href="/wiki/DC_Comics" title="DC Comics">
       DC Comics
      </a>
      characters
      <a href="/wiki/Superman" title="Superman">
       Superman
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Batman" title="Batman">
       Batman
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>

     <p>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Publication_history">
       Publication history
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote boilerplate further">
      Further information:
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_Spider-Man_titles" title="List of Spider-Man titles">
       List of Spider-Man titles
      </a>
     </div>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Creation_and_development">
       Creation and development
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spider_Strikes_v1n1_i05_Wentworth.png">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="926" data-file-width="972" height="210" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Spider_Strikes_v1n1_i05_Wentworth.png/220px-Spider_Strikes_v1n1_i05_Wentworth.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Spider_Strikes_v1n1_i05_Wentworth.png/330px-Spider_Strikes_v1n1_i05_Wentworth.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Spider_Strikes_v1n1_i05_Wentworth.png/440px-Spider_Strikes_v1n1_i05_Wentworth.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spider_Strikes_v1n1_i05_Wentworth.png" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Richard Wentworth a.k.a. the
        <a href="/wiki/Spider_(pulp_fiction)" title="Spider (pulp fiction)">
         Spider
        </a>
        in the pulp magazine
        <i>
         The Spider
        </i>
        . Stan Lee stated that it was the name of this character that inspired him to create a character that would become Spider-Man.
        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeeMair_9-0">
         <a href="#cite_note-LeeMair-9">
          <span>
           [
          </span>
          9
          <span>
           ]
          </span>
         </a>
        </sup>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In 1962, with the success of the
      <a href="/wiki/Fantastic_Four" title="Fantastic Four">
       Fantastic Four
      </a>
      , Marvel Comics editor and head writer
      <a href="/wiki/Stan_Lee" title="Stan Lee">
       Stan Lee
      </a>
      was casting about for a new superhero idea. He said the idea for Spider-Man arose from a surge in teenage demand for comic books, and the desire to create a character with whom teens could identify.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DeFalco_10-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-DeFalco-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :1
      </sup>
      In his autobiography, Lee cites the non-superhuman
      <a href="/wiki/Pulp_magazine" title="Pulp magazine">
       pulp magazine
      </a>
      crime fighter the
      <a href="/wiki/Spider_(pulp_fiction)" title="Spider (pulp fiction)">
       Spider
      </a>
      (see also
      <a href="/wiki/The_Spider%27s_Web" title="The Spider's Web">
       The Spider's Web
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/The_Spider_Returns" title="The Spider Returns">
       The Spider Returns
      </a>
      ) as a great influence,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LeeMair_9-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-LeeMair-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :130
      </sup>
      and in a multitude of print and video interviews, Lee stated he was further inspired by seeing a
      <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">
       spider
      </a>
      climb up a wall—adding in his autobiography that he has told that story so often he has become unsure of whether or not this is true.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        note 1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At that time Lee had to get only the consent of Marvel publisher
      <a href="/wiki/Martin_Goodman_(publisher)" title="Martin Goodman (publisher)">
       Martin Goodman
      </a>
      for the character's approval. In a 1986 interview, Lee described in detail his arguments to overcome Goodman's objections.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        note 2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Goodman eventually agreed to a Spider-Man tryout in what Lee in numerous interviews recalled as what would be the final issue of the science-fiction and supernatural anthology series
      <i>
       Amazing Adult Fantasy,
      </i>
      which was renamed
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Amazing_Fantasy" title="Amazing Fantasy">
        Amazing Fantasy
       </a>
      </i>
      for that single issue, #15 (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cover-date" title="Cover-date">
       cover-dated
      </a>
      August 1962, on sale June 5, 1962).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gcd-af_13-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-gcd-af-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      While this was indeed the final issue, its editorial page anticipated the comic continuing and that "The Spiderman [sic] ... will appear every month in
      <i>
       Amazing
      </i>
      ."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gcd-af_13-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-gcd-af-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
       <a href="#cite_note-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Regardless, Lee received Goodman's approval for the name Spider-Man and the "ordinary teen" concept, and approached artist
      <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kirby" title="Jack Kirby">
       Jack Kirby
      </a>
      . As comics historian
      <a href="/wiki/Greg_Theakston" title="Greg Theakston">
       Greg Theakston
      </a>
      recounts, Kirby told Lee about an unpublished character on which he had collaborated with
      <a href="/wiki/Joe_Simon" title="Joe Simon">
       Joe Simon
      </a>
      in the 1950s, in which an orphaned boy living with an old couple finds a magic ring that granted him superhuman powers. Lee and Kirby "immediately sat down for a story conference", Theakston writes, and Lee afterward directed Kirby to flesh out the character and draw some pages.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theakston_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Theakston-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Steve Ditko would be the inker.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        note 3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      When Kirby showed Lee the first six pages, Lee recalled, "I
      <i>
       hated
      </i>
      the way he was doing it! Not that he did it badly—it just wasn't the character I wanted; it was too heroic".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theakston_15-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Theakston-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :12
      </sup>
      Lee turned to Ditko, who developed a visual style Lee found satisfactory. Ditko recalled:
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       One of the first things I did was to work up a costume. A vital, visual part of the character. I had to know how he looked ... before I did any breakdowns. For example: A clinging power so he wouldn't have hard shoes or boots, a hidden wrist-shooter versus a web gun and holster, etc. ... I wasn't sure Stan would like the idea of covering the character's face but I did it because it hid an obviously boyish face. It would also add mystery to the character....
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ditko-history_17-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ditko-history-17">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         14
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Although the interior artwork was by Ditko alone, Lee rejected Ditko's cover art and commissioned Kirby to pencil a cover that Ditko inked.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gcd-af_13-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-gcd-af-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As Lee explained in 2010, "I think I had Jack sketch out a cover for it because I always had a lot of confidence in Jack's covers."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In an early recollection of the character's creation, Ditko described his and Lee's contributions in a mail interview with Gary Martin published in
      <i>
       Comic Fan
      </i>
      #2 (Summer 1965): "Stan Lee thought the name up. I did costume, web gimmick on wrist &amp; spider signal."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-comicfan2_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-comicfan2-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At the time, Ditko shared a Manhattan studio with noted
      <a href="/wiki/Sexual_fetishism" title="Sexual fetishism">
       fetish
      </a>
      artist
      <a href="/wiki/Eric_Stanton" title="Eric Stanton">
       Eric Stanton
      </a>
      , an art-school classmate who, in a 1988 interview with Theakston, recalled that although his contribution to Spider-Man was "almost nil", he and Ditko had "worked on storyboards together and I added a few ideas. But the whole thing was created by Steve on his own... I think I added the business about the webs coming out of his hands".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Theakston_15-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Theakston-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :14
      </sup>
     </p>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Amazing_Fantasy_15.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="456" data-file-width="300" height="334" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/35/Amazing_Fantasy_15.jpg/220px-Amazing_Fantasy_15.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/35/Amazing_Fantasy_15.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/3/35/Amazing_Fantasy_15.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Amazing_Fantasy_15.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         Amazing Fantasy
        </i>
        #15 (August 1962). The issue that first introduced the fictional character. It was a gateway to the commercial success to the superhero and inspired the launch of
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/The_Amazing_Spider-Man" title="The Amazing Spider-Man">
          The Amazing Spider-Man
         </a>
        </i>
        comics. Cover art by
        <a href="/wiki/Jack_Kirby" title="Jack Kirby">
         Jack Kirby
        </a>
        (penciller) and
        <a href="/wiki/Steve_Ditko" title="Steve Ditko">
         Steve Ditko
        </a>
        (inker).
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Kirby disputed Lee's version of the story, and claimed Lee had minimal involvement in the character's creation. According to Kirby, the idea for Spider-Man had originated with Kirby and
      <a href="/wiki/Joe_Simon" title="Joe Simon">
       Joe Simon
      </a>
      , who in the 1950s had developed a character called the Silver Spider for the
      <a href="/wiki/Crestwood_Publications" title="Crestwood Publications">
       Crestwood Publications
      </a>
      comic
      <i>
       Black Magic,
      </i>
      who was subsequently not used.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        note 4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Simon, in his 1990 autobiography, disputed Kirby's account, asserting that
      <i>
       Black Magic
      </i>
      was not a factor, and that he (Simon) devised the name "Spider-Man" (later changed to "The Silver Spider"), while Kirby outlined the character's story and powers. Simon later elaborated that his and Kirby's character conception became the basis for Simon's
      <a href="/wiki/Archie_Comics" title="Archie Comics">
       Archie Comics
      </a>
      superhero the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fly_(Red_Circle_Comics)" title="Fly (Red Circle Comics)">
       Fly
      </a>
      . Artist
      <a href="/wiki/Steve_Ditko" title="Steve Ditko">
       Steve Ditko
      </a>
      stated that Lee liked the name
      <a href="/wiki/Hawkman" title="Hawkman">
       Hawkman
      </a>
      from
      <a href="/wiki/DC_Comics" title="DC Comics">
       DC Comics
      </a>
      , and that "Spider-Man" was an outgrowth of that interest.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ditko-history_17-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-ditko-history-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Simon concurred that Kirby had shown the original Spider-Man version to Lee, who liked the idea and assigned Kirby to draw sample pages of the new character but disliked the results—in Simon's description, "
      <a href="/wiki/Captain_America" title="Captain America">
       Captain America
      </a>
      with cobwebs".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        note 5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Writer
      <a href="/wiki/Mark_Evanier" title="Mark Evanier">
       Mark Evanier
      </a>
      notes that Lee's reasoning that Kirby's character was too heroic seems unlikely—Kirby still drew the covers for
      <i>
       Amazing Fantasy
      </i>
      #15 and the first issue of
      <i>
       The Amazing Spider-Man
      </i>
      . Evanier also disputes Kirby's given reason that he was "too busy" to also draw Spider-Man in addition to his other duties since Kirby was, said Evanier, "always busy".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evanier_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Evanier-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :127
      </sup>
      Neither Lee's nor Kirby's explanation explains why key story elements like the magic ring were dropped; Evanier states that the most plausible explanation for the sudden change was that Goodman, or one of his assistants, decided that Spider-Man as drawn and envisioned by Kirby was too similar to the Fly.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Evanier_22-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Evanier-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :127
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Author and Ditko scholar Blake Bell writes that it was Ditko who noted the similarities to the Fly. Ditko recalled that, "Stan called Jack about the Fly", adding that "[d]ays later, Stan told me I would be penciling the story panel breakdowns from Stan's synopsis". It was at this point that the nature of the strip changed. "Out went the magic ring, adult Spider-Man and whatever legend ideas that Spider-Man story would have contained". Lee gave Ditko the premise of a teenager bitten by a spider and developing powers, a premise Ditko would expand upon to the point he became what Bell describes as "the first
      <a href="/wiki/Work_for_hire" title="Work for hire">
       work for hire
      </a>
      artist of his generation to create and control the narrative arc of his series". On the issue of the initial creation, Ditko states, "I still don't know whose idea was Spider-Man".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Kirby noted in a 1971 interview that it was Ditko who "got
      <i>
       Spider-Man
      </i>
      to roll, and the thing caught on because of what he did".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Lee, while claiming credit for the initial idea, has acknowledged Ditko's role, stating, "If Steve wants to be called co-creator, I think he deserves [it]".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Writer Al Nickerson believes "that Stan Lee and Steve Ditko created the Spider-Man that we are familiar with today [but that] ultimately, Spider-Man came into existence, and prospered, through the efforts of not just one or two, but many, comic book creators".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26">
       <a href="#cite_note-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_success">
       Commercial success
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      A few months after Spider-Man's introduction, publisher Goodman reviewed the sales figures for that issue and was shocked to find it to have been one of the nascent Marvel's highest-selling comics.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Daniels_27-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Daniels-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :97
      </sup>
      A solo
      <a href="/wiki/Ongoing_series" title="Ongoing series">
       ongoing series
      </a>
      followed, beginning with
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Amazing_Spider-Man" title="The Amazing Spider-Man">
        The Amazing Spider-Man
       </a>
      </i>
      #1 (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cover-date" title="Cover-date">
       cover-dated
      </a>
      March 1963). The title eventually became Marvel's top-selling series
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_3-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wright-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :211
      </sup>
      with the character swiftly becoming a cultural icon; a 1965
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Esquire_(magazine)" title="Esquire (magazine)">
        Esquire
       </a>
      </i>
      poll of college campuses found that college students ranked Spider-Man and fellow Marvel hero the
      <a href="/wiki/Hulk_(comics)" title="Hulk (comics)">
       Hulk
      </a>
      alongside
      <a href="/wiki/Bob_Dylan" title="Bob Dylan">
       Bob Dylan
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Che_Guevara" title="Che Guevara">
       Che Guevara
      </a>
      as their favorite revolutionary icons. One interviewee selected Spider-Man because he was "beset by woes, money problems, and the question of existence. In short, he is one of us."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_3-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wright-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :223
      </sup>
      Following Ditko's departure after issue #38 (July 1966),
      <a href="/wiki/John_Romita,_Sr." title="John Romita, Sr.">
       John Romita, Sr.
      </a>
      replaced him as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Penciler" title="Penciler">
       penciler
      </a>
      and would draw the series for the next several years. In 1968, Romita would also draw the character's extra-length stories in the comics magazine
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Spectacular_Spider-Man#Magazine" title="The Spectacular Spider-Man">
        The Spectacular Spider-Man
       </a>
      </i>
      , a proto-
      <a href="/wiki/Graphic_novel" title="Graphic novel">
       graphic novel
      </a>
      designed to appeal to older readers. It only lasted for two issues, but it represented the first Spider-Man spin-off publication, aside from the original series'
      <a href="/wiki/Annual_publication" title="Annual publication">
       summer annuals
      </a>
      that began in 1964.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      An early 1970s Spider-Man story led to the revision of the
      <a href="/wiki/Comics_Code_Authority" title="Comics Code Authority">
       Comics Code
      </a>
      . Previously, the Code forbade the depiction of the use of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illegal_drugs" title="Illegal drugs">
       illegal drugs
      </a>
      , even negatively. However, in 1970, the
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Nixon" title="Richard Nixon">
       Nixon
      </a>
      administration's
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Department_of_Health,_Education,_and_Welfare" title="Department of Health, Education, and Welfare">
       Department of Health, Education, and Welfare
      </a>
      asked Stan Lee to publish an anti-drug message in one of Marvel's top-selling titles.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_3-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wright-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :239
      </sup>
      Lee chose the top-selling
      <a href="/wiki/Green_Goblin_Reborn!" title="Green Goblin Reborn!">
       <i>
        The Amazing Spider-Man;
       </i>
       issues #96–98
      </a>
      (May–July 1971) feature a
      <a href="/wiki/Story_arc" title="Story arc">
       story arc
      </a>
      depicting the negative effects of drug use. In the story, Peter Parker's friend
      <a href="/wiki/Harry_Osborn" title="Harry Osborn">
       Harry Osborn
      </a>
      becomes addicted to pills. When Spider-Man fights the
      <a href="/wiki/Green_Goblin" title="Green Goblin">
       Green Goblin
      </a>
      (Norman Osborn, Harry's father), Spider-Man defeats the Green Goblin, by revealing Harry's drug addiction. While the story had a clear anti-drug message, the Comics Code Authority refused to issue its seal of approval. Marvel nevertheless published the three issues without the Comics Code Authority's approval or seal. The issues sold so well that the industry's self-censorship was undercut and the Code was subsequently revised.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_3-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wright-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :239
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1972, a second monthly
      <a href="/wiki/Ongoing_series" title="Ongoing series">
       ongoing series
      </a>
      starring Spider-Man began:
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Team-Up" title="Marvel Team-Up">
        Marvel Team-Up
       </a>
       ,
      </i>
      in which Spider-Man was paired with other superheroes and villains. From that point on there have generally been at least two ongoing Spider-Man series at any time. In 1976, his second solo series,
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_Parker,_the_Spectacular_Spider-Man" title="Peter Parker, the Spectacular Spider-Man">
        Peter Parker, the Spectacular Spider-Man
       </a>
      </i>
      began running parallel to the main series. A third series featuring Spider-Man,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Web_of_Spider-Man" title="Web of Spider-Man">
        Web of Spider-Man
       </a>
      </i>
      , launched in 1985 to replace
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Team-Up" title="Marvel Team-Up">
        Marvel Team-Up
       </a>
      </i>
      . The launch of a fourth monthly title in 1990, the "adjectiveless"
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Peter_Parker:_Spider-Man" title="Peter Parker: Spider-Man">
        Spider-Man
       </a>
      </i>
      (with the storyline "
      <a href="/wiki/Torment_(comics)" title="Torment (comics)">
       Torment
      </a>
      "), written and drawn by popular artist
      <a href="/wiki/Todd_McFarlane" title="Todd McFarlane">
       Todd McFarlane
      </a>
      , debuted with
      <a href="/wiki/Variant_cover" title="Variant cover">
       several different covers
      </a>
      , all with the same interior content. The various versions combined sold over 3 million copies, an industry record at the time. Several
      <a href="/wiki/Limited_series" title="Limited series">
       limited series
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/One-shot_(comics)" title="One-shot (comics)">
       one-shots
      </a>
      , and loosely related comics have also been published, and Spider-Man makes frequent
      <a href="/wiki/Cameo_appearance" title="Cameo appearance">
       cameos
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Guest_appearance" title="Guest appearance">
       guest appearances
      </a>
      in other comic series.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_3-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wright-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :279
      </sup>
      In 1996
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Sensational_Spider-Man" title="The Sensational Spider-Man">
        The Sensational Spider-Man
       </a>
      </i>
      was created to replace
      <i>
       Web of Spider-Man
      </i>
      .
     </p>
     <p>
      In 1998 writer-artist
      <a href="/wiki/John_Byrne_(comics)" title="John Byrne (comics)">
       John Byrne
      </a>
      revamped the origin of Spider-Man in the 13-issue limited series
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man:_Chapter_One" title="Spider-Man: Chapter One">
        Spider-Man: Chapter One
       </a>
      </i>
      (Dec. 1998 - Oct. 1999), similar to Byrne's adding details and some revisions to Superman's origin in
      <a href="/wiki/DC_Comics" title="DC Comics">
       DC Comics
      </a>
      '
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Man_of_Steel_(comics)" title="The Man of Steel (comics)">
        The Man of Steel
       </a>
      </i>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Byrne_29-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Byrne-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At the same time the original
      <i>
       The Amazing Spider-Man
      </i>
      was ended with issue #441 (Nov. 1998), and
      <i>
       The Amazing Spider-Man
      </i>
      was restarted with vol. 2, #1 (Jan. 1999).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marvel_500s_30-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Marvel_500s-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In 2003 Marvel reintroduced the original numbering for
      <i>
       The Amazing Spider-Man
      </i>
      and what would have been vol. 2, #59 became issue #500 (Dec. 2003).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Marvel_500s_30-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Marvel_500s-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      When primary series
      <i>
       The Amazing Spider-Man
      </i>
      reached issue #545 (Dec. 2007), Marvel dropped its spin-off ongoing series and instead began publishing
      <i>
       The Amazing Spider-Man
      </i>
      three times monthly, beginning with #546-549 (all Jan. 2008).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The three times monthly scheduling of
      <i>
       The Amazing Spider-Man
      </i>
      lasted until November 2010 when the comic book was increased from 22 pages to 30 pages each issue and published only twice a month, beginning with #648-649 (both Nov. 2010).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The following year, Marvel launched
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Avenging_Spider-Man" title="Avenging Spider-Man">
        Avenging Spider-Man
       </a>
      </i>
      as the first spinoff ongoing series in addition to the still twice monthly
      <i>
       The Amazing Spider-Man
      </i>
      since the previous ones were cancelled at the end of 2007.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <i>
       Amazing
      </i>
      series temporarily ended with
      <a href="/wiki/Dying_Wish" title="Dying Wish">
       issue #700
      </a>
      in December 2012, and was replaced by
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Superior_Spider-Man" title="The Superior Spider-Man">
        The Superior Spider-Man
       </a>
      </i>
      , which had
      <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Octopus" title="Doctor Octopus">
       Doctor Octopus
      </a>
      serve as the new Spider-Man, having taken over Peter Parker's body.
      <i>
       Superior
      </i>
      was an enormous commercial success for Marvel,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
       <a href="#cite_note-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and ran for 31-issue before the real Peter Parker returned in a newly relaunched
      <i>
       The Amazing Spider-Man
      </i>
      #1 in April 2014.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Fictional_character_biography">
       Fictional character biography
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tleft">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Spider-Man_spider-bite.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="366" data-file-width="250" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/86/Spider-Man_spider-bite.jpg/200px-Spider-Man_spider-bite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/86/Spider-Man_spider-bite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/86/Spider-Man_spider-bite.jpg 2x" width="200"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Spider-Man_spider-bite.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The spider bite that gave Peter Parker his powers.
        <i>
         <a href="/wiki/Amazing_Fantasy" title="Amazing Fantasy">
          Amazing Fantasy
         </a>
        </i>
        #15, art by
        <a href="/wiki/Steve_Ditko" title="Steve Ditko">
         Steve Ditko
        </a>
        .
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Forest_Hills,_Queens" title="Forest Hills, Queens">
       Forest Hills, Queens
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">
       New York City
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kempton_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-kempton-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/High_school" title="High school">
       high school
      </a>
      student Peter Parker is a science-whiz orphan living with his
      <a href="/wiki/Uncle_Ben" title="Uncle Ben">
       Uncle Ben
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Aunt_May" title="Aunt May">
       Aunt May
      </a>
      . As depicted in
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Amazing_Fantasy" title="Amazing Fantasy">
        Amazing Fantasy
       </a>
      </i>
      #15 (August 1962), he is bitten by a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radioactive" title="Radioactive">
       radioactive
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Spider" title="Spider">
       spider
      </a>
      (erroneously classified as an
      <a href="/wiki/Insect" title="Insect">
       insect
      </a>
      in the panel) at a science exhibit and "acquires the agility and proportionate strength of an
      <a href="/wiki/Arachnid" title="Arachnid">
       arachnid
      </a>
      ."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Debut_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Debut-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Along with super strength, he gains the ability to adhere to walls and ceilings. Through his native knack for science, he develops a gadget that lets him fire adhesive webbing of his own design through small, wrist-mounted barrels. Initially seeking to capitalize on his new abilities, he dons a costume and, as "Spider-Man", becomes a novelty television star. However, "He blithely ignores the chance to stop a fleeing
      <a href="/wiki/Burglar_(comics)" title="Burglar (comics)">
       thief
      </a>
      , [and] his indifference ironically catches up with him when the same criminal later robs and kills his Uncle Ben." Spider-Man tracks and subdues the killer and learns, in the story's next-to-last caption, "With great power there must also come—great responsibility!"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-daniels95_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-daniels95-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Despite his superpowers, Parker struggles to help his widowed aunt pay rent, is taunted by his peers—particularly
      <a href="/wiki/American_football" title="American football">
       football
      </a>
      star
      <a href="/wiki/Flash_Thompson" title="Flash Thompson">
       Flash Thompson
      </a>
      —and, as Spider-Man, engenders the editorial wrath of
      <a href="/wiki/Newspaper" title="Newspaper">
       newspaper
      </a>
      publisher
      <a href="/wiki/J._Jonah_Jameson" title="J. Jonah Jameson">
       J. Jonah Jameson
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saffel21_39-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-saffel21-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As he battles his enemies for the first time,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gcd_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-gcd-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Parker finds juggling his personal life and costumed adventures difficult. In time, Peter graduates from high school,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and enrolls at
      <a href="/wiki/Empire_State_University" title="Empire State University">
       Empire State University
      </a>
      (a fictional institution evoking the real-life
      <a href="/wiki/Columbia_University" title="Columbia University">
       Columbia University
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/New_York_University" title="New York University">
       New York University
      </a>
      ),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saffel51_43-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-saffel51-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      where he meets roommate and best friend
      <a href="/wiki/Harry_Osborn" title="Harry Osborn">
       Harry Osborn
      </a>
      , and girlfriend
      <a href="/wiki/Gwen_Stacy" title="Gwen Stacy">
       Gwen Stacy
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mnyc_44-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-mnyc-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and Aunt May introduces him to
      <a href="/wiki/Mary_Jane_Watson" title="Mary Jane Watson">
       Mary Jane Watson
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gcd_41-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-gcd-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
       <a href="#cite_note-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saffel27_46-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-saffel27-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      As Peter deals with Harry's drug problems, and Harry's father is revealed to be Spider-Man's nemesis the
      <a href="/wiki/Green_Goblin" title="Green Goblin">
       Green Goblin
      </a>
      , Peter even attempts to give up his costumed identity for a while.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
       <a href="#cite_note-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Gwen Stacy's father,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_York_City_Police" title="New York City Police">
       New York City Police
      </a>
      detective captain
      <a href="/wiki/George_Stacy" title="George Stacy">
       George Stacy
      </a>
      is accidentally killed during a battle between Spider-Man and
      <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Octopus" title="Doctor Octopus">
       Doctor Octopus
      </a>
      (#90, Nov. 1970).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saffel60_49-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-saffel60-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the course of his adventures Spider-Man has made a wide variety of friends and contacts within the superhero community, who often come to his aid when he faces problems that he cannot solve on his own.
     </p>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/The_Night_Gwen_Stacy_Died" title="The Night Gwen Stacy Died">
       issue #121
      </a>
      (June 1973),
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gcd_41-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-gcd-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      the Green Goblin throws
      <a href="/wiki/Gwen_Stacy" title="Gwen Stacy">
       Gwen Stacy
      </a>
      from a tower of either the
      <a href="/wiki/Brooklyn_Bridge" title="Brooklyn Bridge">
       Brooklyn Bridge
      </a>
      (as depicted in the art) or the
      <a href="/wiki/George_Washington_Bridge" title="George Washington Bridge">
       George Washington Bridge
      </a>
      (as given in the text).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      She dies during Spider-Man's rescue attempt; a note on the letters page of issue #125 states: "It saddens us to say that the
      <a href="/wiki/Whiplash_(medicine)" title="Whiplash (medicine)">
       whiplash effect
      </a>
      she underwent when Spidey's webbing stopped her so suddenly was, in fact, what killed her."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saffel65_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-saffel65-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The following issue, the Goblin appears to kill himself accidentally in the ensuing battle with Spider-Man.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GwenDeath_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-GwenDeath-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Working through his grief, Parker eventually develops tentative feelings toward Watson, and the two "become confidants rather than lovers".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sanderson85_54-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Sanderson85-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A romantic relationship eventually develops, with Parker proposing to her in issue #182 (July 1978), and being turned down an issue later.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
       <a href="#cite_note-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Parker went on to graduate from college in issue #185,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gcd_41-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-gcd-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and becomes involved with the shy Debra Whitman and the extroverted, flirtatious costumed thief Felicia Hardy, the
      <a href="/wiki/Black_Cat_(comics)" title="Black Cat (comics)">
       Black Cat
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sanderson83_56-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-sanderson83-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      whom he meets in issue #194 (July 1979).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gcd_41-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-gcd-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      From 1984 to 1988, Spider-Man wore a black costume with a white spider design on his chest. The new costume originated in the
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Secret_Wars" title="Secret Wars">
        Secret Wars
       </a>
      </i>
      <a href="/wiki/Limited_series" title="Limited series">
       limited series
      </a>
      , on an alien planet where Spider-Man participates in a battle between Earth's major superheroes and villains.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He continues wearing the costume when he returns from the Secret Wars, starting in
      <i>
       The Amazing Spider-Man
      </i>
      #252. Not unexpectedly, the change to a longstanding character's iconic design met with controversy, "with many hardcore comics fans decrying it as tantamount to sacrilege. Spider-Man's traditional red and blue costume was iconic, they argued, on par with those of his D.C. rivals Superman and Batman."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cc_58-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-cc-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The creators then revealed the costume was an alien
      <a href="/wiki/Symbiote_(comics)" title="Symbiote (comics)">
       symbiote
      </a>
      which Spider-Man is able to reject after a difficult struggle,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      though the symbiote returns several times as
      <a href="/wiki/Venom_(comics)" title="Venom (comics)">
       Venom
      </a>
      for revenge.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gcd_41-5">
       <a href="#cite_note-gcd-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Parker proposes to Watson a second time in
      <i>
       The Amazing Spider-Man
      </i>
      #290 (July 1987), and she accepts two issues later, with
      <a href="/wiki/The_Wedding!_(comics)" title="The Wedding! (comics)">
       the wedding
      </a>
      taking place in
      <i>
       The Amazing Spider-Man Annual
      </i>
      #21 (1987)—promoted with a real-life mock wedding using actors (with model Tara Shannon as Watson)
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      at
      <a href="/wiki/Shea_Stadium" title="Shea Stadium">
       Shea Stadium
      </a>
      , with Stan Lee officiating, on June 5, 1987.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saffel124_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-saffel124-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wedding_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wedding-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However,
      <a href="/wiki/David_Michelinie" title="David Michelinie">
       David Michelinie
      </a>
      , who scripted based on a plot by editor-in-chief
      <a href="/wiki/Jim_Shooter" title="Jim Shooter">
       Jim Shooter
      </a>
      , said in 2007, "I didn't think they actually should [have gotten] married. ... I had actually planned another version, one that wasn't used."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-saffel124_61-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-saffel124-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In a controversial storyline, Peter becomes convinced that
      <a href="/wiki/Ben_Reilly" title="Ben Reilly">
       Ben Reilly
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Scarlet_Spider" title="Scarlet Spider">
       Scarlet Spider
      </a>
      (a clone of Peter created by his college professor
      <a href="/wiki/Jackal_(Marvel_Comics)" title="Jackal (Marvel Comics)">
       Miles Warren
      </a>
      ) is the real Peter Parker, and that he, Peter, is the clone. Peter gives up the Spider-Man identity to Reilly for a time, until Reilly is killed by the returning Green Goblin and revealed to be the clone after all.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Life_of_Reilly_63-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Life_of_Reilly-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In stories published in 2005 and 2006 (such as "
      <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man:_The_Other" title="Spider-Man: The Other">
       The Other
      </a>
      "), he develops additional spider-like abilities including biological web-shooters, toxic stingers that extend from his forearms, the ability to stick individuals to his back, enhanced Spider-sense and night vision, and increased strength and speed. Peter later becomes a member of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/New_Avengers_(comics)" title="New Avengers (comics)">
       New Avengers
      </a>
      , and reveals his civilian identity to the world,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      furthering his already numerous problems. His marriage to Mary Jane and public unmasking are later erased in another controversial
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OMDPart1p1_65-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-OMDPart1p1-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      storyline "
      <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man:_One_More_Day" title="Spider-Man: One More Day">
       One More Day
      </a>
      ", in a
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Faustian_bargain" title="Faustian bargain">
       Faustian bargain
      </a>
      with the demon
      <a href="/wiki/Mephisto_(comics)" title="Mephisto (comics)">
       Mephisto
      </a>
      , resulting in several adjustments to the timeline, such as the resurrection of Harry Osborn, the erasure of Parker's marriage, and the return of his traditional tools and powers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OneMoreDay_66-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-OneMoreDay-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      That storyline came at the behest of editor-in-chief
      <a href="/wiki/Joe_Quesada" title="Joe Quesada">
       Joe Quesada
      </a>
      , who said, "Peter being single is an intrinsic part of the very foundation of the world of Spider-Man".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OMDPart1p1_65-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-OMDPart1p1-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It caused unusual public friction between Quesada and writer
      <a href="/wiki/J._Michael_Straczynski" title="J. Michael Straczynski">
       J. Michael Straczynski
      </a>
      , who "told Joe that I was going to take my name off the last two issues of the [story] arc" but was talked out of doing so.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OMDPart2p1_67-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-OMDPart2p1-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      At issue with Straczynski's climax to the arc, Quesada said, was
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       ...that we didn't receive the story and methodology to the resolution that we were all expecting. What made that very problematic is that we had four writers and artists well underway on [the sequel arc] "Brand New Day" that were expecting and needed "One More Day" to end in the way that we had all agreed it would. ... The fact that we had to ask for the story to move back to its original intent understandably made Joe upset and caused some major delays and page increases in the series. Also, the science that Joe was going to apply to the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Retcon" title="Retcon">
        retcon
       </a>
       of the marriage would have made over 30 years of Spider-Man books worthless, because they never would have had happened. ...[I]t would have reset way too many things outside of the Spider-Man titles. We just couldn't go there....
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-OMDPart2p1_67-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-OMDPart2p1-67">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         62
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      Following the "reboot", Parker's identity was no longer known to the general public; however, he revealed it to his teammates in the New Avengers
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and his friends in the Fantastic Four, and others have deduced it. Parker's Aunt May married J. Jonah Jameson's father, Jay Jameson.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
       <a href="#cite_note-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Jonah himself has been elected Mayor of New York City,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and Parker became an employee of the think-tank Horizon Labs.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Dying_Wish" title="Dying Wish">
       issue #700
      </a>
      , after the dying supervillain
      <a href="/wiki/Doctor_Octopus" title="Doctor Octopus">
       Doctor Octopus
      </a>
      has swapped bodies with him, Parker dies.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, the ending of
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Superior_Spider-Man" title="The Superior Spider-Man">
        The Superior Spider-Man
       </a>
      </i>
      #1 it shows that Parker still exists within Doctor Octopus's mind.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      It is later revealed that the Peter Parker persona within Doctor Octopus's mind were just memories that he absorbed from Peter Parker,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      which he later purges to be completely free to control Parker's former body.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
       <a href="#cite_note-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      However, Peter's consciousness is revealed to still exist, having somehow escaped the mind-purge from Doctor Octopus, but Peter decided to keep a low profile to prevent Doctor Octopus from attempting to erase him again.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Doctor Octopus found himself overwhelmed while battling the Green Goblin with few resources or allies. Realizing he had failed as the "superior" Spider-Man he claimed to be, Doctor Octopus let the consciousness of Parker reclaim his body.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Following Peter Parker's return,
      <i>
       The Amazing Spider-Man
      </i>
      was re-launched in April 2014.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79">
       <a href="#cite_note-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80">
       <a href="#cite_note-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Parker's consciousness has returned to its body but he has no memories of what Doctor Octopus did while in his body.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Parker begins to deal with the fallout of Doctor Octopus' possession.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Personality">
       Personality
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width: 33%; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
      <div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
       <div>
        <p>
         "People often say glibly that Marvel succeeded by blending super hero adventure stories with soap opera. What Lee and Ditko actually did in
         <i>
          <a href="/wiki/The_Amazing_Spider-Man" title="The Amazing Spider-Man">
           The Amazing Spider-Man
          </a>
         </i>
         was to make the series an ongoing novelistic chronicle of the lead character's life. Most super heroes had problems no more complex or relevant to their readers' lives than thwarting this month's bad guys.... Parker had far more serious concern in his life: coming to terms with the death of a loved one, falling in love for the first time, struggling to make a living, and undergoing crises of conscience."
        </p>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div style="text-align: left;">
       Comics historian
       <a href="/wiki/Peter_Sanderson" title="Peter Sanderson">
        Peter Sanderson
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         78
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      As one contemporaneous journalist observed, "Spider-Man has a terrible identity problem, a marked
      <a href="/wiki/Inferiority_complex" title="Inferiority complex">
       inferiority complex
      </a>
      , and a fear of women. He is
      <a href="/wiki/Antisocial_personality_disorder" title="Antisocial personality disorder">
       anti-social
      </a>
      ,  [
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">
        sic
       </a>
      </i>
      ]
      <a href="/wiki/Castration" title="Castration">
       castration
      </a>
      -ridden, racked with
      <a href="/wiki/Oedipus_complex" title="Oedipus complex">
       Oedipal guilt
      </a>
      , and accident-prone ... [a] functioning
      <a href="/wiki/Neurosis" title="Neurosis">
       neurotic
      </a>
      ".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kempton_36-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-kempton-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Agonizing over his choices, always attempting to do right, he is nonetheless viewed with suspicion by the authorities, who seem unsure as to whether he is a helpful vigilante or a clever criminal.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-daniels96_84-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-daniels96-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Notes cultural historian Bradford W. Wright,
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       Spider-Man's plight was to be misunderstood and persecuted by the very public that he swore to protect. In the first issue of
       <i>
        The Amazing Spider-Man
       </i>
       , J. Jonah Jameson, publisher of the
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Daily_Bugle" title="Daily Bugle">
         Daily Bugle
        </a>
       </i>
       , launches an editorial campaign against the "Spider-Man menace." The resulting negative publicity exacerbates popular suspicions about the mysterious Spider-Man and makes it impossible for him to earn any more money by performing. Eventually, the bad press leads the authorities to brand him an outlaw. Ironically, Peter finally lands a job as a photographer for Jameson's
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Daily_Bugle" title="Daily Bugle">
         Daily Bugle
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_3-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-Wright-3">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         3
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :212
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <p>
      The mid-1960s stories reflected the political tensions of the time, as early 1960s Marvel stories had often dealt with the
      <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">
       Cold War
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Communism" title="Communism">
       Communism
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_3-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wright-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       :220–223
      </sup>
      As Wright observes,
     </p>
     <blockquote class="templatequote">
      <p>
       From his high-school beginnings to his entry into college life, Spider-Man remained the superhero most relevant to the world of young people. Fittingly, then, his comic book also contained some of the earliest references to the politics of young people. In 1968, in the wake of actual militant
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Student_demonstration" title="Student demonstration">
        student demonstrations
       </a>
       at Columbia University, Peter Parker finds himself in the midst of similar unrest at his Empire State University.... Peter has to reconcile his natural sympathy for the students with his assumed obligation to combat lawlessness as Spider-Man. As a law-upholding liberal, he finds himself caught between militant leftism and angry conservatives.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wright_3-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-Wright-3">
         <span>
          [
         </span>
         3
         <span>
          ]
         </span>
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">
        :234–235
       </sup>
      </p>
     </blockquote>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Powers.2C_skills.2C_and_equipment">
       Powers, skills, and equipment
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man%27s_powers_and_equipment" title="Spider-Man's powers and equipment">
       Spider-Man's powers and equipment
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      A bite from a radioactive spider on a school field trip causes a variety of changes in the body of Peter Parker and gives him superpowers.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_85-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Science-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the original
      <a href="/wiki/Stan_Lee" title="Stan Lee">
       Lee
      </a>
      -
      <a href="/wiki/Steve_Ditko" title="Steve Ditko">
       Ditko
      </a>
      stories, Spider-Man has the ability to cling to walls, superhuman strength, a sixth sense ("spider-sense") that alerts him to danger, perfect balance and equilibrium, as well as superhuman speed and agility. Some of his comic series have him shooting webs from his wrists.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_85-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Science-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Academically brilliant, Parker has expertise in the fields of
      <a href="/wiki/Applied_science" title="Applied science">
       applied science
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Chemistry" title="Chemistry">
       chemistry
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">
       physics
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Biology" title="Biology">
       biology
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">
       engineering
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Mathematics" title="Mathematics">
       mathematics
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Mechanics" title="Mechanics">
       mechanics
      </a>
      . The character was originally conceived by Stan Lee and Steve Ditko as intellectually gifted, but not a genius; however, later writers have depicted the character as a genius.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      With his talents, he sews his own
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spider-Man%27s_costumes" title="Spider-Man's costumes">
       costume
      </a>
      to conceal his identity, and constructs many devices that complement his powers, most notably mechanical web-shooters.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Science_85-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Science-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      This mechanism ejects an advanced adhesive, releasing web-fluid in a variety of configurations, including a single rope-like strand to swing from, a net to bind enemies, and a simple glob to foul machinery or blind an opponent. He can also weave the web material into simple forms like a shield, a spherical protection or hemispherical barrier, a club, or a hang-glider wing. Other equipment include spider-tracers (spider-shaped adhesive homing beacons keyed to his own spider-sense), a light beacon which can either be used as a flashlight or project a "Spider-Signal" design, and a specially modified camera that can take pictures automatically.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_versions">
       Other versions
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Alternative_versions_of_Spider-Man" title="Alternative versions of Spider-Man">
       Alternative versions of Spider-Man
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      Due to Spider-Man's popularity in the mainstream
      <a href="/wiki/Marvel_Universe" title="Marvel Universe">
       Marvel Universe
      </a>
      , publishers have been able to introduce different variations of Spider-Man outside of mainstream comics as well as reimagined stories in many other
      <a href="/wiki/Multiverse_(Marvel_Comics)" title="Multiverse (Marvel Comics)">
       multiversed
      </a>
      spinoffs such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Ultimate_Spider-Man" title="Ultimate Spider-Man">
        Ultimate Spider-Man
       </a>
      </i>
      ,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man_2099" title="Spider-Man 2099">
        Spider-Man 2099
       </a>
      </i>
      , and
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man:_India" title="Spider-Man: India">
        Spider-Man: India
       </a>
      </i>
      . Marvel has also made its own parodies of Spider-Man in comics such as
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Not_Brand_Echh" title="Not Brand Echh">
        Not Brand Echh
       </a>
      </i>
      , which was published in the late 1960s and featured such characters as Peter Pooper alias Spidey-Man,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and Peter Porker, the Spectacular
      <a href="/wiki/Spider-Ham" title="Spider-Ham">
       Spider-Ham
      </a>
      , who appeared in the 1980s. The fictional character has also inspired a number of deratives such as a
      <a href="/wiki/Spider-Man:_The_Manga" title="Spider-Man: The Manga">
       manga version of Spider-Man
      </a>
      drawn by
      <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">
